<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="2a8c71e09f3a40d9bde9c91149319c8a">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '743a8d8e-20dd-4207-b896-755c2a07014f'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d128664368" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are entering commercial relevance as a sustainable and low-cost alternative to lithium-ion batteries. Improving the energy density of SIBs is critical to enable their widespread adoption. Here, a new class of cathode materials Na<sub>6</sub><i>M</i>S<sub>4</sub> (<i>M</i> = Co, Mn, Fe, and Zn) that exhibit high charge-storage capacity is reported. Using Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> as a prototypical example, a six-electron conversion reaction dominated by anion redox is observed, confirmed through various electrochemical and spectroscopic techniques. After the initial cycle, Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> delivers a high capacity of 392&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with a long lifespan of over 500 cycles. The reaction involves, initially, the transformation of crystalline Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> to a nearly amorphous structure consisting of mainly CoS and sulfur nanoparticles, which then reversibly cycles between nearly amorphous a-CoS/S and a-Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>. Such anion-redox-driven conversion-type cathodes hold the potential to enable energy-dense, stable SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202403521-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202403521-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In the realm of energy storage, the rapid evolution of battery technologies has ignited a fervent quest for safer, more efficient, sustainable solutions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0001_R_d128664355e380" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Commun. Nat. Energy Nat. Energy Adv. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For a battery chemistry to enter the market for energy storage applications such as electric vehicles (EVs), they need to satisfy some critical parameters, such as an energy density of &gt; 150&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> with &gt; 500 cycles. As lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) continue to dominate the market, concerns about the availability and cost of lithium resources have spurred interest in alternative battery chemistries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0002_R_d128664355e387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among the emerging contenders, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have recently crossed this threshold and have begun to be employed in EVs due to their potential to offer performance comparable to LIBs; while, utilizing more abundant sodium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0003_R_d128664355e393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Energy Nat. Energy Nat. Commun.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cathode, a pivotal component of SIBs, plays a crucial role in determining the overall performance and characteristics of the batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0004_R_d128664355e398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For many years, cathode materials for SIBs mainly relied solely on transition metal (cationic) redox, resulting in a limited capacity and energy density (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0001">1a</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0005_R_d128664355e410" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Sustain. Nat. Energy">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is critical to look into approaches that can enhance significantly the energy density and lifespan of SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202403521-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d7a58fef-e96c-46cb-9b7d-e5a4117ef27f/adma202403521-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d7a58fef-e96c-46cb-9b7d-e5a4117ef27f/adma202403521-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/92ec0ed6-a99f-4f5f-a6bc-dea7a4da9ba0/adma202403521-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d7a58fef-e96c-46cb-9b7d-e5a4117ef27f/adma202403521-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202403521-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202403521" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Cathode design strategies. a) Illustration of the advantages of anion-redox cathodes. b) The comparative electrochemical performance of the reported cathodes for Na ion batteries.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In recent years, sulfur cathodes with a conversion redox reaction have emerged as a potential candidate for high-energy-density SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0006_R_d128664355e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, such cathodes with a conversion redox reaction suffer from unstable redox chemistry during the conversion, including the shuttling effect, leading to a limited lifespan.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0007_R_d128664355e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Apart from classical intercalation chemistry and conversion chemistry, there are materials that are a hybrid, that is, anionic-redox materials. While oxygen-based anionic redox is interesting from an energy-density perspective, significant challenges arise from the electrolyte stability in the presence of peroxide and superoxide intermediate species. Hence, there has been immense interest in recent years in anionic redox in LIBs with the electrochemical participation of sulfur to offer higher capacities, such as Li-rich layered sulfides Li<sub>1.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.57</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>S<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0008_R_d128664355e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Energy">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Historically, it is well-known that FeS<i><sub>x</sub></i> and MoS<i><sub>x</sub></i> can undergo anion redox. However, these materials do not contain sodium; and thus, would necessitate pairing them with a Na-metal anode, which is known to have poor reversibility. Therefore, it is preferable to have a cathode that contains sodium in it as it can allow the use of traditional anodes such as hard carbon, or even anode-free cell designs. An early effort by Shadike and co-workers reported reversible S<sup>2−</sup>/S<sub>2</sub><sup>2−</sup> redox in layered O3-type NaCrS<sub>2</sub> cathode,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0009_R_d128664355e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Commun.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which delivered a capacity of 103 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. NaCrS<sub>2</sub> with anionic sulfur redox provided new insights to bring forth high-capacity cathodes for SIBs. In such materials, sulfur existed fully or partially as dimerized S<sup>−</sup>─S<sup>−</sup> (or S<sub>2</sub><sup>2−</sup>) pairs that broke into the standard S<sup>2−</sup> state upon electrochemical insertion of Na (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0001">1b</a>). However, the anionic redox activity was only partly reversible in such materials, resulting in rapid capacity fade.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0010_R_d128664355e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Mater. Chem. A">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, there is much appetite to develop stable cathodes with high capacities for SIBs, potentially involving anionic redox.</p>
         
         <p>We present here a new class of ternary transition-metal sulfides (Na<sub>6</sub>MS<sub>4</sub>), including Na<sub>6</sub>MnS<sub>4</sub>, Na<sub>6</sub>FeS<sub>4</sub>, Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>, and Na<sub>6</sub>ZnS<sub>4</sub>, as cathodes for SIBs with high capacities. These materials are known to display interesting magnetic and optical properties,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0011_R_d128664355e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chemistry J. Alloys Compd. Z. Anorg. Allg. Chem. Z. Anorg. Allg. Chem.">11</a>, <a href="#adma202403521-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0012_R_d128664355e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> but their electrochemical properties are not known. In this work, we first present a facile way to synthesize Na<sub>6</sub><i>M</i>S<sub>4</sub> (<i>M</i> = Mn, Fe, Co, and Zn) via a carbothermal reaction. Unlike the conventional layered oxide cathodes used in LIBs and SIBs, that rely exclusively on the redox of transition metals (cationic), the reversible reaction of Na<sub>6</sub>MS<sub>4</sub> involves an anionic redox process. They exhibit remarkably high reversible capacities of close to 400 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a long cycle life, following an initial six-electron conversion reaction involving a transformation from highly crystalline to an amorphous structure, which shows great potential compared to most published cathodes for SIBs, as demonstrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0001">1b</a>.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202403521-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202403521-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>For SIBs, there are three major classes of inorganic cathode materials. A schematic showing their operation mechanisms is given in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0001">1a</a>. The specific capacities, energy densities, and cycle life of some common SIB cathode materials are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0001">1b</a> and are detailed in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information. The first class of SIB cathode materials is intercalation compounds— these have a host crystal structure with channels for Na<sup>+</sup> ions to be inserted and extracted (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0001">1a</a>, left). Examples include transition-metal oxides, sulfides, and polyanionic materials wherein the active redox center is the transition-metal ion. As the host structure tends to be stable, they are rechargeable for a few hundred to a few thousand cycles. Unfortunately, the multi-atomic nature of the host structure and a redox reaction with one or less than one electron transfer per transition-metal ion translates to a low theoretical capacity, typically &lt; 200 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and despite a high operating voltage (&gt;3&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) to a low theoretical energy density of &lt; 600&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0001">1b</a>).</p>
         
         <p>The other class of inorganic cathode materials is elemental cathodes, such as sulfur and oxygen. They typically undergo conversion directly from their elemental form to their reduced form through multi-electron reactions on a lightweight substrate, such as carbon (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0001">1a</a>, center); and thus, possess a very high capacity &gt; 1000 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>; despite a lower voltage (between 1.5 and 3&nbsp;V), they have a theoretical energy density of &gt; 1500&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. However, these conversion reactions are sluggish and inefficient and often involve parasitic reactions. Consequently, the cycle life is poor, typically ranging from a few tens to a couple of hundred cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0001">1b</a>).</p>
         
         <p>At the intersection of these two material classes are hybrid or anion-redox-based cathodes. These are transition-metal oxides or sulfides, but with the anions acting as the active redox species. These materials possess a host crystal structure that could undergo rearrangements to facilitate the conversion of the anions within a matrix (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0001">1a</a>, right). Therefore, the reactions are not sluggish and are efficient. Consequently, a life of a few hundred cycles can be obtained. As the anions undergo multi-electron reactions, capacities between 300 and 500 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be obtained. As they inherit the best features of both intercalation and conversion chemistries, they are attractive candidates as inorganic cathode materials.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">A facile scalable synthesis of Na<sub>6</sub>MS<sub>4</sub> is critical for the practical application in SIBs. Several preparation methods have been reported in the literature,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0011_R_d128664355e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chemistry J. Alloys Compd. Z. Anorg. Allg. Chem. Z. Anorg. Allg. Chem.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> starting with air-sensitive chemicals, such as Na<sub>2</sub>S, which cannot be directly processed in air. Therefore, we developed a facile carbothermal reduction of metal sulfates with carbon to obtain Na<sub>6</sub>MS<sub>4</sub>, as in Equation (<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adma202403521-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>).

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma202403521-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/9909e212-5e94-475c-901b-5842071c7bf4/adma202403521-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202403521:adma202403521-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adma202403521-math-0001.png">
                     <semantics>

                        </mtable>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray}M{\mathrm{S}}{{{\mathrm{O}}}_4} &amp;+&amp; 3{\mathrm{ N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}_2}{\mathrm{S}}{{{\mathrm{O}}}_4} + 8{\mathrm{C}} \to {\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}_6}M{{{\mathrm{S}}}_4}\nonumber\\ &amp;+&amp; 8{\mathrm{C}}{{{\mathrm{O}}}_2}\left( {M{\mathrm{ = Mn, Fe, Co, and Zn}}} \right)\end{eqnarray}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>In Equation (<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adma202403521-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>), excess carbon was employed to ensure a complete reduction of the sulfates and ensure efficient conversion. Any unreacted, remaining carbon would also serve to construct a conductive network within the cathode. The procedure is detailed below by taking Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> as an example. Initially, all the reactants (Ketjen Black, Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>, and CoSO<sub>4</sub>) were ultrasonically mixed in water and ethanol and transferred to a rotary evaporator to dry the mixture. Following a high-temperature treatment of the mixture at 800 °C under nitrogen, Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> was obtained. A similar procedure was adopted with <i>M</i> = Co, Fe, Mn, and Zn in Na<sub>6</sub>MS<sub>4</sub>. Such a one-pot synthesis ensured a molecular-level mixing of the reactants, leading to the formation of nanosized Na<sub>6</sub>MS<sub>4</sub> particles in the carbon matrix (Na<sub>6</sub>MS<sub>4</sub>@C).</p>
         
         <p>As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0002">2a</a>, Na<sub>6</sub>MS<sub>4</sub> crystallizes in the hexagonal <i>P6<sub>3</sub>mc</i> space group. The structure is comprised of a hexagonal closed-packed sulfur lattice in which the M<sup>2+</sup> ions occupy the tetrahedral sites, forming MS<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra; while, the Na<sup>+</sup> ions occupy both the tetrahedral and octahedral sites equally, creating two inequivalent Na<sup>+</sup> sites in a 1:1 ratio. In the resulting structure, the NaS<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra share faces; while, the NaS<sub>6</sub> octahedra share edges with the MS<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0011_R_d128664355e781" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chemistry J. Alloys Compd. Z. Anorg. Allg. Chem. Z. Anorg. Allg. Chem.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The structure of Na<sub>6</sub>MS<sub>4</sub>@C is investigated with scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3b,c</a>, Supporting Information, the Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C composite comprises nanoparticles clustered in secondary particles of ≈1&nbsp;µm that are interconnected to form a 3D porous network. The distribution of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C is further revealed by the elemental mapping images in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information, suggesting that Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> is uniformly distributed along with carbon. The XRD pattern in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information confirms the high crystallinity of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> (PDF#86-1254). In addition, we also prepared the Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> according to the previously reported method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0011_R_d128664355e822" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chemistry J. Alloys Compd. Z. Anorg. Allg. Chem. Z. Anorg. Allg. Chem.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information, the prepared sample shows impurity phases, such as Na<sub>5</sub>Co<sub>2</sub>S<sub>5</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>S. These results further confirm that our method ensures a molecular-level mixing of the reactants, leading to a uniform reaction. All the other samples, including Na<sub>6</sub>FeS<sub>4</sub>@C, Na<sub>6</sub>MnS<sub>4</sub>@C, and Na<sub>6</sub>ZnS<sub>4</sub>@C, also show structures similar to Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C, as seen in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6–S14</a>, Supporting Information.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202403521-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/93efd681-83bf-4575-85e4-e4ad58b8a5fc/adma202403521-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/93efd681-83bf-4575-85e4-e4ad58b8a5fc/adma202403521-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9a42ec79-ad8e-4d3d-912e-bbb495458b45/adma202403521-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/93efd681-83bf-4575-85e4-e4ad58b8a5fc/adma202403521-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202403521-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202403521" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Redox behavior of Na<sub>6</sub>MS<sub>4</sub>. a) Crystal structure of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>. b) Redox energies of the transition-metal ions with respect to the top of the S<sup>2−</sup>:2p band. c) Evolution of pDOS with various transition metals. The Na, S, Zn, Mn, Co, and Fe projections are colored, respectively, in red, grey, light green, purple, blue, and yellow. d) Voltage profiles of the compositions synthesized versus Na metal at C/10 rate between 1.2 and 2.8&nbsp;V in the first cycle.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0002">2b</a>, amongst the transition metal ions, Co<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> should be a good choice for sulfides because the Co<sup>2+/3+</sup>: 3d and Fe<sup>2+/3+</sup>: 3d redox couples are expected to be pinned at the top of the S<sup>2−</sup>: 3p band. The spin-projected density of states (pDOS) for Na<sub>6</sub>ZnS<sub>4</sub>, Na<sub>6</sub>MnS<sub>4</sub>, Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>, and Na<sub>6</sub>FeS<sub>4</sub> are calculated, and the results are plotted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0002">2c</a>. According to the Zaanen–Sawatzky–Allen theory, the charge transfer term ∆ is the energy difference between the ligand bonding (M─S) and antibonding (M─S)<sup>*</sup> orbitals that come from the hybridization of transition-metal d orbitals and the p-orbitals of S, corresponding to the ionic-covalent character of M─S bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0013_R_d128664355e929" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chemistry">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The results reveal that Na<sub>6</sub>ZnS<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>6</sub>MnS<sub>4</sub> have a wider bandgap compared to Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>6</sub>FeS<sub>4</sub> due to the differences in the location of the M:3d-band with respect to the top of the S:3p band, suggesting that both Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>6</sub>FeS<sub>4</sub> would have better electronic conductivity.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performances of the Na<sub>6</sub>MS<sub>4</sub>@C samples were evaluated in Na half cells, and the voltage profiles are summarized in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0002">2d</a>. Na<sub>6</sub>ZnS<sub>4</sub>@C shows inferior electrochemical activity as only two Na<sup>+</sup> ions can be reversibly extracted. The redox activity in Na<sub>6</sub>ZnS<sub>4</sub>@C is clearly due to the anion redox of S<sup>2−</sup> as Zn<sup>2+</sup> with a 3d<sup>10</sup> configuration would not be expected to participate or form Zn<sup>3+</sup>. In addition, the limited redox activity in Na<sub>6</sub>ZnS<sub>4</sub>@C is due to the lying of Zn<sup>2+/3+</sup> energy well above the top of the S<sup>2−</sup>: 2p band and lack of covalent mixing between Zn:3d and S:2p orbitals (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0002">2b,c</a>). This contrasts with Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C and Na<sub>6</sub>FeS<sub>4</sub>@C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0002">2c</a>), which have much larger electrochemical activity due to the high covalence of the M─S bond, resulting from a significant overlap of the M<sup>2+/3+</sup>: 3d band with the top of the S<sup>2−</sup>: 2p band (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0002">2a</a>).</p>
         
         <p>The cycling performances of Na<sub>6</sub>ZnS<sub>4</sub>@C, Na<sub>6</sub>MnS<sub>4</sub>@C, Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C, and Na<sub>6</sub>FeS<sub>4</sub>@C are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0003">3a</a>. Both Na<sub>6</sub>ZnS<sub>4</sub>@C and Na<sub>6</sub>MnS<sub>4</sub>@C cathodes show large irreversible capacity and their capacities quickly fade below 50 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after five cycles due to the lack of covalence. In contrast, Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C and Na<sub>6</sub>FeS<sub>4</sub>@C show good reversible cycling performance. For discussion, we select Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C as a model system to further demonstrate the potential of ternary transition-metal sulfides. The Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode delivers a high initial charge specific capacity of 477&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1C rate (1C = 495 mA g<sup>-1</sup>), which corresponds to ≈ 5.8 Na<sup>+</sup> ions extracted from Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C. Then, 5.3 Na<sup>+</sup> ions can be inserted reversibly, which corresponds to a discharge capacity of 437&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. The discharge capacity becomes stable at 392&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> in the subsequent cycles. The fast decay in the first two cycles is mainly due to the transformation of highly crystalline Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> to an amorphous structure and cathode–electrolyte interphase (CEI) formation, which will be further discussed later. Even after 500 cycles, Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C maintains a capacity of 306&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to a capacity fade rate of 0.04% per cycle. In addition, Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C exhibits an average Coulombic efficiency of 99.6% over 500 cycles at 0.1C rate, indicating a high reversibility of the conversion and the good structural integrity during repeated cycling. In addition, the cobalt-free Na<sub>6</sub>FeS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode delivers a high initial charge specific capacity of 440&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C rate, which corresponds to ≈5.3 Na<sup>+</sup> ions extracted from Na<sub>6</sub>FeS<sub>4</sub>@C. Then, 5.0 Na<sup>+</sup> ions can be inserted reversibly, which corresponds to a discharge capacity of 416&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. The discharge capacity becomes stable at 401&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> in the subsequent cycles, and the Na<sub>6</sub>FeS<sub>4</sub>@C maintains a capacity of 232&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 185 cycles.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202403521-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/32efbb30-f536-499d-a09f-70946226807a/adma202403521-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/32efbb30-f536-499d-a09f-70946226807a/adma202403521-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/99e9e2cc-6051-4958-9917-aef08f1fc555/adma202403521-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/32efbb30-f536-499d-a09f-70946226807a/adma202403521-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202403521-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202403521" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical behavior of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C. a) Long-term cycling performance of Na<sub>6</sub>MS<sub>4</sub>@C cathodes. b) The corresponding discharge/charge voltage profiles of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode. c) Rate performance of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode at various C rates. d) Cycling performance of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C coin cell with a high active material loading (5&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>). Cycling performances of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C pouch cell with e) a low active material loading and f) a high active material loading and lean electrolyte conditions. g) Cycling performances of anode-free Ni|| Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cell.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode for the first six cycles in the voltage range of 1.2 to 2.8&nbsp;V with a scanning rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. A strong anodic peak at 2.27&nbsp;V is detected in the initial scanning, which can be attributed to the transformation of the crystalline Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> to an amorphous structure, and finally, to S/CoS. The cathodic peak at 1.52&nbsp;V is associated with the conversion of S/CoS back to Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>. After the first sweep, the overlapping CV curves indicate a good reversibility of the conversion.</p>
         
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0003">3b</a> shows the charge/discharge profiles of the Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode at 0.1C&nbsp;rate. The voltage plateaus match well with the CV results in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information. Only a slight change in the potential plateau, even after 500 cycles, confirms the high-capacity reversibility of the Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0004">4c</a>, the rate capability of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C is conducted at various C rates. Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C exhibits excellent rate capability with high reversible specific capacities of 418, 354, 312, 283, 240, 197, and 156 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at, respectively, 0.1 C, 0.2 C, 0.5 C, 1 C, 1.5 C, 2 C, and 4 C rates. Notably, the capacity recovers to 386 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> as the C rate is reduced from 4 C to 0.1 C, indicating the stability of the Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode at high rates.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202403521-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/73b4a972-9789-4e44-8b57-f93f8364ceee/adma202403521-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/73b4a972-9789-4e44-8b57-f93f8364ceee/adma202403521-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a3ab3128-9ff2-416b-b299-f0dc2d45149c/adma202403521-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/73b4a972-9789-4e44-8b57-f93f8364ceee/adma202403521-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202403521-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202403521" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Behavior of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C. a) Charge and discharge curves of a Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C coin cell in the initial cycle and b) the corresponding diffraction patterns. c) CV curves of a Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C coin cell in the first two cycles and d) the corresponding time sequence of Raman spectra.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode was further evaluated under a high active material (AM) loading of 5&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup> and a low electrolyte/AM ratio of 7&nbsp;µL mg<sup>−1</sup> to demonstrate the practical viability of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0003">3d</a>, the cell with a high Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> loading and a low electrolyte amount practically maintained a cycling performance similar to the cell with a low Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> loading, demonstrating the outstanding redox kinetics of the Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>.</p>
         
         <p>The excellent performance of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode with high AM loading motivated us to explore its viability in pouch cells, which is an important step toward attaining practical SIBs. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0003">3e</a>, the pouch cell with a Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C loading of 2&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup> and a low electrolyte/AM ratio of 7&nbsp;µL mg<sup>−1</sup> delivered an initial specific capacity of 341 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Even after 200 cycles, the pouch cell maintained a high specific capacity of 293 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, the most extended cycling lifespan of sodium-ion pouch cells, as summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information. It should be noted that after the initial ten cycles, the pouch cell showed almost no capacity decay upon cycling. To further pursue the practical viability of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode in pouch cell, the loading was further improved to 5.3&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, and the electrolyte amount was further reduced to 4&nbsp;µL mg<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0003">3f</a>, the pouch cell with a high loading could deliver a high initial capacity of 357 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and remain stable over 60 cycles. The results further illustrate the stability of the Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode.</p>
         
         <p>To further demonstrate the advantage of using the sodium-containing Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode, an anode-free cell was constructed with a localized high-concentration electrolyte (LHCE) and Ni foil as the anode current collector for Na deposition. In detail, Na was deposited onto the Ni current collector during charge, and the Na on the Ni current collector was stripped off and returned to the cathode during discharge. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0003">3g</a>, the Ni||Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cell could still deliver an initial discharge capacity of 408&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, which was very close to that of the Na||Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cell paired with a Na-foil anode, suggesting an efficient deposition of Na in an anode-free Ni||Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cell. The Ni||Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C anode-free cell maintained 187&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> over 40 cycles, demonstrating a highly reversible reaction of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> with a high utilization of Na in the anode-free Ni||Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cell.</p>
         
         <p>The voltage profiles in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0002">2d</a> indicate a significant potential difference between the first de-sodiation step (charge) and the first sodiation step (discharge) of the Na<sub>6</sub>MS<sub>4</sub> class of materials. No change in the shape of the charge/discharge curve was observed in the subsequent cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0003">3b</a>). This suggests crystalline rearrangements in Na<sub>6</sub>MS<sub>4</sub> during first cycle. This phenomenon was further investigated using a suite of in- situ and in- operando techniques, such as synchrotron-based operando energy dispersive X-ray diffraction (ED-XRD) and Raman spectroscopy with Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode as the prototypical compound as it appears to be the most electrochemically reversible sample. ED-XRD was used as the ultrahigh-energy of the X-ray (up to 200&nbsp;keV) at the sector 6BM-A of the Advanced Photon Source in Argonne National Laboratory, providing a deep penetration depth, allowing for identifying the structural changes in the cathode using normal coin cells (CR2032) for the measurement.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0014_R_d128664355e1422" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0004">4a</a>, the cell was tested at 0.1C rate between 1.2 and 2.8&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) for one full discharge–charge cycle. In the meantime, the XRD signal was recorded, as demonstrated in the contour plot (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0004">4b</a>) next to the charge–discharge profile. In situ Raman spectroscopy was performed to gain an understanding of the transformations after the amorphization of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0004">4c</a>, the cell was tested at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> in the range of 1.2–2.8&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) for two cycles. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0004">4d</a> shows the time-resolved Raman image recorded on the cathode material during cycling.</p>
         
         <p>In addition to in situ techniques, ex- situ analysis, such as TEM and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> at different states of cycling were conducted to investigate the detailed product phase. As described earlier, based on the covalent nature of the Co─S bonds owing to the overlap of the Co 3d band with the S 2p bands, the removal of electrons/sodium from the structure would result in an oxidation of the sulfide anions. In the contour plot of the ED-XRD data in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0004">4b</a>, a set of dominant peaks of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> (PDF#86-1254) with strong intensity could be observed at the initial stage of the charge process. As the charge step proceeded, the diffraction peak intensity tended to weaken. The first peak to lose detectable intensity corresponded to the (301) plane of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>, suggesting the desodiation began with the removal of sodium from the NaS<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra. The next peak to lose intensity belonged to the (220) plane, indicating sodium removal from the octahedral site. At ≈80% depth-of-charge, all the peaks of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>, except the ones for (101) and (211) planes, completely vanished, suggesting the transformation of the MS<sub>4</sub> tetrahedral unit to a MS<sub>6</sub> octahedra, which is prevalent in CoS.</p>
         
         <p>This loss of intensity is corroborated by the TEM images (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0005">5</a>), wherein the size of the primary Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> particles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0005">5a</a>) changed from several 100&nbsp;nm size grains to 4–8&nbsp;nm size grains (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0005">5b–d</a>). In addition, TEM also showed 2–4&nbsp;nm size domains of CoS and S wrapped in the carbon matrix, confirming the observation of CoS in ED-XRD. XPS was used to track the oxidation state changes through this process. The S 2p spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0005">5f</a>) of the fresh sample showed two sulfur contributions at 159.8 and 162.4&nbsp;eV, corresponding to, respectively, the Na─S bonds and Co─S bonds. When the Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C was fully charged, the peaks in the S 2p spectrum showed a positive shift with two sulfur contributions at 162.5 and 164.1&nbsp;eV, attributed, respectively, to the Co─S bonds and S─S bonds; this indicates the formation of sulfur and CoS, as seen in the TEM, and validates that the sulfide anions are oxidized to sulfur through anion redox. This also implies that there is little change in the oxidation state of Co. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information, the fresh Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C sample shows two Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> components at 779.5 and 782.9&nbsp;eV, corresponding, respectively, to Co<sup>2+</sup> and Co<sup>2+</sup> satellites. After fully charging, only Co<sup>2+</sup> and Co<sup>2+</sup> satellites are observed albeit with a positive shift of 0.5&nbsp;eV owing to the formation of CoS and its subsequent interaction with S. Over the course of charging, it appears that there is no net oxidation state change in Co, which indicates that the oxidation of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C could be through cation-assisted anion redox.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202403521-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0eac1564-4d38-4a55-b358-fea742d1a19c/adma202403521-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0eac1564-4d38-4a55-b358-fea742d1a19c/adma202403521-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ff5155ce-8563-423f-b70e-49f3a75d8389/adma202403521-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0eac1564-4d38-4a55-b358-fea742d1a19c/adma202403521-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202403521-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202403521" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterizations of cycled Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C. a) High-resolution TEM images of fresh Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C, b–d) fully charged Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C, and e) fully discharged Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C. f) S 2p spectra of the fresh, fully charged, and fully discharged Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The discharge starts with the cathode having nanometer-sized domains of CoS and S. For this reason, it is hard to see any peaks in the ED-XRD. To resolve this issue, we rely on in situ Raman spectroscopy, wherein the pristine Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> can be seen in the 1200–1650&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> range.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0015_R_d128664355e1580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Mater. Chem. A">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While no peaks are observed in the charged state, during the reduction process, the intensity of the typical peaks of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> gradually becomes strong. In addition, the peaks of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> become both broad and intense in the second cycle, suggesting the loss of long-range translational symmetry and implying the formation of nanosized Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>. TEM of the discharged cathode sheds light into both the morphology and identity of the cathode in the discharged state. The discharged cathode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0005">5e</a>) contains a mosaic of 8–10&nbsp;nm size grains of Na<sub>2</sub>S and Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). The elemental mapping images of the fresh and fully discharged samples in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information suggest that Na and S, along with Co are present. However, the presence of Na<sub>2</sub>S and the broadening of peaks in the Raman spectra suggest that a small portion of the sulfur may be segregated during the first charge step, which converts back to Na<sub>2</sub>S; while, the sulfur close to CoS can become amorphous Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> (a-Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>). The loss of sulfur from the parent Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> during the first cycle to yield Na<sub>2</sub>S and the sulfur-deficient a-Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> are also corroborated by the XPS data of the discharged cathode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0005">5f</a>), showing a higher intensity for the Na─S peaks compared to Co─S; while, the Co oxidation state remains +2 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). The formation of Na<sub>2</sub>S and a-Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> explains the differences in the voltage curve and the loss of some reversible capacity between the first and the second cycle.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Based on the evidence from the different materials characterization techniques, we deem the unique Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> reaction pathway as described below:

                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray}{\mathrm{Subsequent \  charge}}&amp;:&amp;{\mathrm{ a}} - {\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}_6}{\mathrm{Co}}{{{\mathrm{S}}}_4} + x{\mathrm{ N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}_2}{\mathrm{S}}\nonumber\\ &amp;&amp;\to \left( {6 + 2x} \right){\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + } + 3{\mathrm{S}} + {\mathrm{CoS}} + \left( {6 + 2x} \right){{{\mathrm{e}}}^ - }\end{eqnarray}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>This reaction pathway has been schematically represented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202403521-fig-0006">6</a>. The class of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> displays a six-electron redox reaction. During the initial charge process, Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> first transforms from a highly crystalline to an amorphous structure, mainly consisting of nanosized CoS and sulfur nanoparticles. Afterward, the conversion is highly reversible from amorphous a-CoS/S to a-Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> and some Na<sub>2</sub>S particles with rich, small, disordered crystal domains. The amorphous particles formed during the cycling process of NaCoS<sub>4</sub> can provide more active reactive sites and reduce the energy barrier for ion diffusion; thus, improving cycling performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0016_R_d128664355e1909" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202403521-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/461a10cf-27c0-453e-ba0d-1e15c6e0e41d/adma202403521-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/461a10cf-27c0-453e-ba0d-1e15c6e0e41d/adma202403521-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c29bf210-42f0-4d3b-991f-6779c4aca059/adma202403521-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/461a10cf-27c0-453e-ba0d-1e15c6e0e41d/adma202403521-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202403521-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202403521" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic of the conversion of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C. A conversion from high crystallinity to an amorphous structure.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>This anion-redox-based conversion reaction possesses several advantages. First, unlike the conventional sodium–sulfur batteries, the Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> cathode redox does not involve sodium polysulfide (NaPSs) formation and shuttling, overcoming the capacity decay challenges of conventional sulfur cathodes. Such a conclusion can be easily confirmed by the good cycling performance of Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode in the conventional low concentration electrolyte of 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaFSI in DME, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information, compared to the few cycles lifespan observed in the literature for sulfur cathode in low-concentration electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202403521-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202403521-bib-0012_R_d128664355e1954" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Second, conductivity changes in the material through cycling were traced through the evolution of pDOS during the desodiation process, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information. Following the Na removal, there was a depletion of the Co 3d band and the S 3p band near the Fermi level, indicating the co-oxidation of both Co<sup>2+</sup> and S<sup>2-</sup> during charge, resulting from the overlap of the Co<sup>2+/3+</sup> and S<sup>2−</sup>: 2p bands and the strong hybridization and pinning of the Co:3d and S:2p orbitals. We also found that the conductivity of Na<sub>6−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>CoS<sub>4</sub> became better during the Na extraction process due to the introduction of holes into the S<sup>2−</sup>:3p band, which was beneficial for electrode performance. In addition, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information, the differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) profiles with LHCE and carbonate electrolyte after charge were compared. The Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode with carbonate electrolyte showed two weak exothermic peaks at 141 °C and 213 °C at the beginning, followed by two strong exothermic peaks at 267 °C and 304 °C. The exothermic heat amount increased with the increase of temperature, and an obvious thermal runaway problem occurred. The Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode with LHCE showed only a weak heat absorption (endotherm) at 112 °C and a faint exotherm at 218 °C. Therefore, the thermal stability of the Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode was good, and there was a high tolerance for thermal runaway problems with the LHCE.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202403521-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202403521-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, for the first time, a class of Na<sub>6</sub><i>M</i>S<sub>4</sub> (M = Mn, Fe, Co, and Zn) is shown to serve as cathode material for Na-ion batteries. By selecting Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> as a model system, a unique cathode chemistry is discovered. During the initial charge process, Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> first transforms from a highly crystalline to an amorphous structure, mainly consisting of nanosized CoS and sulfur nanoparticles. Afterward, the conversion is highly reversible from amorphous a-CoS/S to a-Na<sub>6</sub>CoS<sub>4</sub> particles with rich, small disordered crystal domains. The six-electron redox reaction is realized through such a unique conversion pathway, whose theoretical capacity is 495&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. Finally, when we compare the theoretical energy of different classes of Na-cathodes, including polyanion, layered oxide, and Prussian blue analogs, as summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information, the Na<sub>6</sub>MS<sub>4</sub>@C cathode family delivers the energy density of 926&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

    window.addEventListener('load', () => {
        document.querySelectorAll('.admin-bar-popup-menu ul li')?.forEach(function (li) {
